## Thread: Spelling — 7 msgs

### Re: Spelling

- **From:** "Donald Tees" <donald@willmack.com>
- **Date:** 1998-07-02T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6nhhf4$7eg$1@news.igs.net>`
- **References:** `<6ncemf$3uq$1@news.igs.net> <359A7A79.13316D3E@onramp.net> <6nedvu$h62$1@news.igs.net> <359b947a.1513285@news.generation.net>`

```

>The same way that I would.  I am Canadian, and am
>set up for English rather than American spelling. That
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>Canada is, after all, a distinct society <g>.

mais oui

Several years back, I did s contract for a major US company
that had been bought out by a major French company.  It seems
that the new parent had decided that all code would be replaced
by the French systems.  Unfortunately, all documentation was in
French.

In their wisdom, the American counterparts (in a fit of pique actually)
realized that they had not a person that understood a word of French
so they sent it all up to Brampton, Ontario.  They knew, you see, that
everybody in Canada spoke French.

In addition, it was the worst designed system I have ever seen.  By the
time I got there, they had not cut an invoice in seven months, and had
lost about 90 million dollars worth on inventory(shipped, but who knew
where? only Honeywell Bull, and it was not telling).

After spending three weeks commuting between Toronto and the US
(both side sabotaging everything I did, in full cover your ass mode, like
if the 600 of us cannot get it working nobody can), I finally scrounged
a backup tape, and managed to dump it to cards.  I sorted them in
order on a card sorter I found ware-housed (They promised me
computer time in 8-10 weeks), I printed the cards on unit record equipment
and highlighted the appropriate columns, and set a dozen typists to work.

Three weeks later, I placed 87 million dollars worth of manually typed
invoices on the president's desk, and told him to fired the entire data
processing department, both countries.  Their budget was in the millions,
I did it with typists for under twenty grand.

I have always kept in mind since that politics is more important than code
in systems design.
```

## Thread: COBOL 3 DEBUG TOOL — 1 msgs

### Re: COBOL 3 DEBUG TOOL

- **From:** David Abere <da2@columbia.edu>

One more stupid headhunter trick:

Don't call me or email me and ask if I know somebody else with
experience in whatever.  We all know you're calling me to solicit me. 
It's okay to add "do you know somebody else?" later, but starting there
is a lie and starts me feeling suspicious about you. 
```

#### ↳ Re: Any crash courses in Cobol in New York state?

- **From:** steve wolfe <prgsdw@epix.net>
- **Date:** 1998-07-11T00:00:00

```
rc2000nospam@ibm.net wrote:
> 
> Hello, and thanks for your indulgence. With the shortage of programmers
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
> Please remove "nospam" to e-mail me.

Hmmm....I'm starting to get real cynical on this "programmer shortage"
issue. We interview a lot of people at my shop and there is always some
total BS reason why talented people are passed over. I've been told
"they only know Expeditor and we use Intertest, therefor they aren't
'ready to hit the ground running'". What BS. Or they have been doing
only DB2/COBOL II for the last 5 years - implying that they aren't
capible of learning CICS. So instead of having a proven (ie more
expensive) programmer on board, we'll hire a college grad (ie cheaper)
and put him or her through a 3 month training course and then teach them
on the job for at least another year to get them productive - all
because we don't want to give that experienced programmer a couple weeks
to come up to speed with basic CICS. It just doesn't make sense. Or the
usual - well they just wanted $XXXXX per year and that's as much as
manager's make here! 

So we have teams that are literally running the company that have 4 or 5
people left where 2 years ago there was 20. The project I'm on is
"highest priority" for moving the company forward strategically and
there are 8 people out of an alloted 21 - and two of them have already
accepted offers with other companies. And we are deemed at almost full
staff...It's going to be interesting what type of glass ceiling
corporate america throws up for the year 2000 programmers when they
start to apply back with corporations - I think that there will be quite
a grudge held against them - wrongly so, but it will be there none the
less. I've heard people make comments like 'we'll see what they will be
making back here in a couple of years'.

Seems like there are plenty of people out there - it is a matter of
finding people who will work cheaply enough and long enough that is the
issue. We seem to be much more willing to give an unproven programmer a
chance to learn everything about programming in the real world than to
pay an experienced programmer to come in and learn our environment. Is
this just in my area of the country or is this type of double speak
going on everywhere? 

Steve
```

## Thread: PL/I memories, was: Looking for COBOL source recovery — 2 msgs

### Re: PL/I memories, was: Looking for COBOL source recovery

- **From:** no.spam@this.net (Mail: Y2000 infomagic com)
- **Date:** 1998-07-03T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.software.year-2000,eit.mirror.cobol-lovers,fj.lang.cobol,alt.cobol,comp.lang.pl1,comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<00AF25F4E282A373.704C344BA3D5D05D.638CFFE0987EEB6C@library-proxy.airnews.net>`
- **References:** `<MPG.ffb298a5510a9b9989683@news.globalserve.net> <6ncmf5$9qj81@reader1.wxs.nl> <i21+XNAa5fm1EwKK@elmbronze.demon.co.uk> <359a455f.140220174@news.nbnet.nb.ca> <39A14C753E0717B4.3976069378DC254D.DA79191863C8CC53@library-proxy.airnews.net> <7kepWhCNP4qd-pn2-Xd8ucM1idLph@localhost>`

```

On 2 Jul 1998 10:51:02 GMT, kiyoinc@ibm.XOUT.net (cory hamasaki)
wrote:

>I also tried writing a TCAM control address space in PL/I using the 
>Transient/Pending support, ended up doing it in assembler, better TCAM
>macro support.

My first PL/I project was similar.  I wrote a TP control program in
Assembler using a German queueing system called GENA (roughly
equivalent to TCAM).  I wrote the terminal control, messaging and
database management in Assembler, but the application layer was all in
PL/I.  So I learned an awful lot about generated object code before I
even wrote my first PL/I program!  (Later I worked on a PL/I compiler
for a micro system).

My first "real" PL/I program was a statistical analysis of the
performance, transaction and error logs I'd built into the system.  I
remember revelling in the power of PL/I compared to Cobalt, and I
still have fond memories of a single IF statement which flowed across
4 pages of the listing -- just to decide whether or not to skip to a
new page.  The things we did as kids!

All of this ran under DOS on a 360/40 with 128K.  Response time was
sub second and I still ran two batch partitions and full spooling.

I ran the upgrade to a 370, but left before the app was converted to
CICS.  I heard later that response time increased to 3 seconds, which
really made my day!

>I wrote a TPUT/TGET interface for PL/I, a couple pages of assembler, and
>used it to write a full screen desk calculator in PL/I; did the 3270 
>attributes, SBA, etc. in PL/I.

At one point I wrote a series of Assembler macros to handle the 3270
data streams under CICS.  It worked much better than IBM's BMS,
allowed for much easier screen definition and totally minimized the
actual data stream.  Remember when _that_ was important -- you know
when 2400 bps was _fast_?  Wasn't that when we had to worry about
saving a couple of bytes here and there by . . .  Oh _that's_ how this
Y2K date-rape business got started <G>.

>I never liked the PLI checkout compiler, it always seemed too slow on a 
>2 MIPS box.  It seemed easier to run the optimizing compiler and 
>sprinkle in printf's ... ah, I mean put data's and put edits.

C, anyone can make that mistake . . .

>The OPEN/370 has an emulated 3330 an MVT SYSRES, so I have IFOX00,
>IEUASM, and IEMAA.    If I had a few weeks, I'd be moving MVT and MVS
>code off the OPEN/370 to the XT/370s.  

Ah yes, but is it compliant <G>?

>Why?  The OPEN/370 is too expensive for a knock around machine, it's 
>just as pricey as a P390.  I don't like hauling it to clients for demos,
>taking it out to the weekend place.

Is the OPEN/370 that one from Funsoft or am I having a senior moment?
How do you like it?

>When I say not right now, please keep me informed.  There's lots of 
>really strange stuff going on.  Don't forget, if you decide to get a 
>P390, go through S390 DEVCON.  

I'll keep you informed (and those who have contacted me privately).

Natch on the DEVCON.  I would have already set that up, but I think
it's probably easier to get Commercial 390 Devcon if I go from my OS/2
devcon membership and I left that number back home in Arizona.

If we get enough real heavyweights interested, I might even be able to
come up with some vulture capital.

>Good discussion Infomagic, :Dave, thanks for the trip down memory lane.
>I've decided to ease up on the denial-heads, you two and I have too much
>work to do... and certainly they've figured out that there's a couple
>dozen or so c.s.y2k'ers who are cranking code.

In case you hadn't noticed, I don't follow on to many of them either.
It's not worth the effort any more.  Evolution in action!

I've seen the same sort of denial when I teach multi-engine flying.
One of the things I like to do is simulate an engine failure
immediately after liftoff from a runway with trees at the end.  The
flight manual says the plane will climb on one, and it does.  But not
fast enough to clear those trees.

You have no idea how difficult it is to sit there watching the trees
move UP in the windshield (meaning we're not going to clear them).
Eventually a look of sheer and abject terror appears on their face as
they realize that there's _nothing_ they can do to keep us from dying
in a great ball of fire.  When I see that look I bring the power back
on the "dead" engine and let him climb on out.  Don't say anything,
though.  The silence says it all.

(Note to other instructors:  Don't do this without proper preparation
and safety procedures.  I'll gladly send you mine).

_Next_ time I _guarantee_ the student knows in advance that he can't
make it, he accepts this and lands on the remaining runway, safely,
even if that would mean running the plane off the runway and perhaps
even totaling it.  But the occupants would survive.

Y2K pollyannas are a lot like this.  They're flying into the trees on
one.  After all it's still flying, and they know the procedures for
dealing with the problem (i.e. fixing bits of code).  They just can't
understand that losing 50% of the (processing) power means losing 80%
of the climb/economic performance.  And they just won't listen to a
more experienced instructor!

=====================================
y 2 0 0 0 @ i n f o m a g i c . c o m
=====================================
The good news is we're way ahead of everybody else!
The bad news is we're way ahead of everybody else!
```

I did wonder what the correct procedure was in a twin.  I think if it

grin on his face, and his hand on the throttle.  I guess they were used
to it there, as not one head turned upwards.  I often wondered if it was
for real, and we lurched to a halt having wheelbarrowed into a bunker,
if the greenkeeper would come rushing out with a big rake in his hand,
and insist we cleared up the mess.

:Dave

## Thread: Looking for COBOL source recovery — 2 this month / 13 total

_Thread started [1998-06](1998-06.md#t-cf7856b6b2)._

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Looking for COBOL source recovery

- **From:** gvwmoore@ix.netcom.com (G Moore)
- **Date:** 1998-07-03T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<35a4b022.15357524@nntp.ix.netcom.com>`
- **References:** `<MPG.ffb298a5510a9b9989683@news.globalserve.net> <6oyYgEAMjhk1Ew4C@elmbronze.demon.co.uk> <7kepWhCNP4qd-pn2-5VVL7odLZlEK@localhost>`

```

On 25 Jun 1998 23:55:25 GMT, kiyoinc@ibm.XOUT.net (cory hamasaki)
wrote:

>9) Is Assembler source acceptable or are you stuck on COBOL?

hmm. i would go for C, instead of assembler or COBOL.
the reason is simple. there are more C programmers
out there which can figure out what the re-written
source code is. the disadvantage of C is that
the code will not be as fast as assembler. but you
will be able to fix the problem within a time frame
at a cheaper price.

the thing with C is, if the machine has a stack, you
have to make utterly sure that you have access
to the push and pop commands in the compiler. another
thing to watch for is re-entrancy.

most machines already have decompilers that creates C
code. its not a good decompiler, but the hard part will
have to be done by human hands anyways.

and teaching alot of people a way to write assembler for
each type of computer at this point in time is not a
wise thing to do, especially if they have to learn
the specifics of the machine they are working on.


gvwmoore@ix.spam.netcom.com to reply remove the spam
```

You *really* need to have a COBOL programmer who is knowledgeable
in the source system perform the data extraction. There are flavors of
COBOL, and even if you can code in COBOL the file contents could be
misinterpreted by your compiler.

Michael D. Long
http://www.geocities.com/siliconvalley/heights/9748/

The (brand of) the source system is important. COBOL is a
language and ISAM is a generic technique. There must be tools in
the original workbench for dumping (propriety) ISAM files to text.
It may be hidden as an option somewhere (backup, sort, ...).

Paul

<a id="t-ca3cf8f35b"></a>
## Thread: Follow up: Question for CICS gurus -- CICS CALL statements] — 1 msgs

_Topics: [`Mainframe, z/OS, JCL, CICS`](topics.md#mainframe)_

### Re: Follow up: Question for CICS gurus -- CICS CALL statements]

- **From:** Bill Lynch <wblynch@att.net>
- **Date:** 1998-07-03T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** alt.cobol,comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6nhmbp$gq0@bgtnsc03.worldnet.att.net>`
- **References:** `<359BB445.FA569E2A@earthlink.com> <6ngt57$4fn$1@heliodor.xara.net> <6ngtik$cgk@bgtnsc01.worldnet.att.net>`

```

Ramone wrote:
> 
(snip)
> No.  This merely bypasses the load from //STEPLIB.  You would never be
> allowed to do this in my shop, but if you insist, then you must have
> your program in a //STEPLIB library, not a //DFHRPL library.

No, it's a static call. On completion of the link edit (or Binder) step,
the load module will contain the module called "PROGRAM". The CICS
region's STEPLIB and/or DFHRPL don't enter into it. What matters is the
SYSLIB concatenation for the link edit (or Binder) step, meaning
"PROGRAM" had better be found there. This is true in an MVS 5.2. &
CICS/ESA 4.1 environment. If you believe otherwise please describe your
operating environment's attributes.

Also, if you would not permit a static call, what did you do regarding
calling subroutines from OS/VS COBOL programs (assuming, of course, that
you had OS/VS COBOL programs)?

Bill Lynch

> >
> >    Would appreciate any feed back.  I promise to post any updates or solutions to this problem.
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> >
```

<a id="t-903f778b7d"></a>
## Thread: Follow up: Question for CICS gurus -- CICS CALL statements] — 1 msgs

_Topics: [`Mainframe, z/OS, JCL, CICS`](topics.md#mainframe)_

### Re: Follow up: Question for CICS gurus -- CICS CALL statements]

- **From:** "Cobol Frog (Huib Klink)" <H.Klink@IMN.nl>
- **Date:** 1998-07-03T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** alt.cobol,comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<359C77A3.CA2BC6D1@IMN.nl>`
- **References:** `<359BB445.FA569E2A@earthlink.com> <359C3A5F.31C4@sprynet.com>`

```

Frank Swarbrick wrote:

> When saying CALL PGMID USING RECORD you are doing a dynamic call.

8<

> When saying CALL 'PROGRAM' USING RECORD you are doing a static call.

8<

> Hope this is all correct...

Most probably. But it is possible to tell the compiler that the second (CALL 'PROGRAM')
also should be dynamic. In most situations (AFAIK them) this option (DYNAM) is NOT set, so
the difference between static and dynamic can be programmed by using CALL literal and CALL
identifier respectively. But you can not swap that reasoning if the option is set. (When
all frogs are green, it does not follow that every green thing is a frog)

The COBOL green thing

> --
> Frank Swarbrick
> home: infocat@sprynet.com
> work: frank.swarbrick@1stbank.com
```

## Thread: How to get address of Cobol variable? — 1 msgs

_Topics: [`Help requests and how-to`](topics.md#help)_

I remember one IBM COBOL version in which one could only use the special registers ADDRESS OFF var
(aov). There were lots of rules, like the aov of any field (w-storage and linkage) could be sending in
the set ... to aov, but only the linkage fields could be receiving in set addres of linkageSectionItem
to something. Also the rule that the oav of linkage items as receivers only could be 01 levels etc.etc.

Perhaps this was one of the first versions of IBM's compiler for COBOL 85 (called COBOL II)?

already possible in their first COBOL II compiler?

<a id="t-aa5ec5e6e8"></a>
## Thread: How to get address of Cobol variable? — 2 msgs

_Topics: [`Help requests and how-to`](topics.md#help)_

### Re: How to get address of Cobol variable?

- **From:** "COBOLFrog (Huib Klink)" <H.Klink@IMN.nl>
- **Date:** 1998-07-03T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<359C80CD.C83B3025@IMN.nl>`
- **References:** `<01bda426$68ac5980$703d868b@nmc-pc> <1998070213375500.JAA18163@ladder01.news.aol.com> <359BACB4.6B6B@bt.com>`

```

Patrick Herring wrote:

> S Comstock wrote:
> ...
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> 31-bit addressing,

On IBM mainframes, this bit is not used for *addressing* and as such
normally set to zero although it could be one, because it is ignored by the
hardware address registers. To keep things neat, it als almost always set to
zero.BUT ...
The bit is used for some totally other thing in this IBM blue world. And
most frequently used that way:
When passing a parameter list (CALL ... USING P1, P2 etc) in machine code
there is a register involved that is filled by the caller with the addes of
the list of addresses of the parameters. Say for example that there is a
list of three addresses, that will be 12 bytes long (3 parms X 4 bytes).
These 12 bytes (contiguous) are in storage on some location and THAT address
is put in the parameter-list-address-register (in COBOL usually register 1,
I am told). Now how does at run-time now the called program how many
addresses are in the list, in other words how many parameters are passed?
Simply by the wide-spread convention that all addresses except the last will
have that one bit ZERO. The leftmost bit is thus an end-of-list-flag.
Other compilers, f.e. PL/1, use other addressing schemes, f.e. passing the
address of an parameter-description block in which much more info is present
like number of parameters, type and size of them (especially for arrays and
structures) a.s.o.

> and does the sign in the picture mean Cobol does the
> Right Thing with it?

(This stays typically IBM)In COBOL there is no way to describe a binary
field in base 2. The picture is always base-10. The number of 9's is telling
the maximum decimal value: 10**8-1 in this case. This is stored binary,
because usage is specified as such (COMP). Now the compiler reasons as
follows:
1) I am allowed to allocate either 2, 4 or 8 bytes for binary fields
2) I try the smallest possible which is able to hold the figure 10**8-1 in
binary format.
The result is a 4 bytes field. In that field, you may know (but need not
know) how the info is stored. If you use it to store an address, you
probably do not want to know the internal structure, but knowing that the
SIGN is part of the 32 bits, you specify it, so all buts will be usable.
Most probably the compiler thas not move around with the bits, so the sign
is not really needed. But you never know. This is implementor defined.

If you want to display the address, you can also redefine it as pic x(4) and
use some charToHex function to show it hexadecimally on the screen.

The Frog
```

#### ↳ Re: How to get address of Cobol variable?

- **From:** RandallBart <Barticus@att.spam.net>
- **Date:** 1998-07-03T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6njdhv$k6d@bgtnsc01.worldnet.att.net>`
- **References:** `<01bda426$68ac5980$703d868b@nmc-pc> <1998070213375500.JAA18163@ladder01.news.aol.com> <359BACB4.6B6B@bt.com> <359C80CD.C83B3025@IMN.nl>`

```

COBOLFrog (Huib Klink) wrote:
> 
> Patrick Herring wrote:

> > and does the sign in the picture mean Cobol does the
> > Right Thing with it?
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
> is not really needed. But you never know. This is implementor defined.

Actually, the standard expects the compiler to always keep the value
within the PIC.  If you move an address of X"7FFF0000" to a PIC 9(8)
field, the decimal value 134213632 should be truncated to 34213632
(X"20A0F00").  I understand that there's some compile time kluge to get
around this.  In A Series we have USAGE BINARY EXTENDED (not for
addresses, just to avoid truncation).
```

## Thread: Solve this terminology argument — 1 msgs

### Re: Solve this terminology argument

- **From:** "COBOLFrog (Huib Klink)" <H.Klink@IMN.nl>
- **Date:** 1998-07-03T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<359C8385.C94EBBD1@IMN.nl>`
- **References:** `<35979F3F.6FCE@netvision.net>`

```



Yosh Mantinband wrote:

> Can you solve an argument for me?
>
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> is that called a Reference Modifier or a Substring? or something else?

SOMETHING is called an identifier(2:5) is called "reference modifier".
(you know the [] should be ()).
The USE of these combined is called "reference modification".
The RESULT of this combination is called "subset". The new standard says:
"reference modification creates a unique data item that is a subset of
...".

> Why do you think that's what it's called?

Because that's in the standard.

> How about the fields in a statement, e.g.,
>
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> or what?

"sending operand" and "receiving operand", sometines the standard uses
"receiving item" or "receiving data item".

> Thanks,
> Yosh

For curiousity, WHY asked this?

The COBOL Frog
```

Klint Kendrick wrote:

> Another thing that might bring my neophyte status into the light is the fact that
> I actually thought you were talking about headhunters in this thread rather than
> agencies.  Silly me.

My first encounter with employment agencies was back in 1976.  Back then
the word "headhunter" was quite pejorative.  Most would be offended if
called "headhunter", and less than 10% would use the term themselves.

By the mid-1980s the word "headhunter" was in common use.  Many agents
openly called themselves headhunters, and probably more than 90% would
(if asked) smile and say "Yes, I'm a headhunter".  

Now it's become the standard term.  There's not a shred of pejorative
left, and in order to be insulting we call you "pimp".  (If the client
screws me and doesn't pay me, will you go beat him up?  If not, then
you're not a pimp.)

If "headhunter" is now a legally defined term in the jurisdiction where
you operate, then use the word as required by your headhunting license,
but otherwise we mean anyone who makes their money matching people with
jobs, whether they make their money in a lump sum when I start a job (or
90 days later) or as an hourly percentage.  Note that the commission is
what makes the headhunter -- being paid by the head.  Someone who works
in the personnel office of a company is a headhunter if and only if they
get paid commissions.

Agents of headhunting companies are called headhunters even when they
work on salary.  Such people are actually apprentice headhunters trying
to work their way up to commissioned or partnership positions.
```

### I need help with a signed field

- **From:** wwalters@ionert.net (Willie)

                                          pic 9(10) and
                                          pic 9

- **Date:** 1998-07-04T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6nk409$kn0_001@p12-term2-and.netdirect.net>`

```

In article <6njq1m$3s4$1@ionews.ionet.net>,
   wwalters@ionert.net (Willie) wrote:
+I haven't programmed in COBOL for several years....now I have a little
+project that requires COBOL and it seems I have forgotten a lot!

03 level) and you're doing your MOVE at the group level (01).

MOVE command(s) in the Procedure Division.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: I need help with a signed field

- **From:** redsky@ibm.net (Thane Hubbell)
- **Date:** 1998-07-06T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<Jl0PnHJ5PvPd-pn2-pfmHrvurVw4o@Dwight_Miller.iix.com>`

```

On Sat, 4 Jul 1998 03:26:38, dlmiller@inetdirect.net (Doug Miller) 
wrote:

> In article <6njq1m$3s4$1@ionews.ionet.net>,
>    wwalters@ionert.net (Willie) wrote:

```

#### ↳ Re: I need help with a signed field

- **From:** riplin@kcbbs.gen.nz (Richard Plinston)
- **Date:** 1998-07-04T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3298184.17793.27694@kcbbs.gen.nz>`
- **References:** `<6njq1m$3s4$1@ionews.ionet.net>`

```

In message <<6njq1m$3s4$1@ionews.ionet.net>> wwalters@ionert.net  writes:
> project that requires COBOL and it seems I have forgotten a lot!
> 
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> field.   What am I doing wrong?

It sounds like you are doing the move to a group field, ie:

      01   Com-3-field           PIC S9(11) COMP-3.
      01   Group-Field.
           03  Display-10-field   PIC 9(10).
           03  Display-1-field    PIC 9.

           MOVE Com-3-Field    TO Group-Field

will treat the move as an alphanumeric move, thus the first few
bytes will just be the packed data and the PIC 9 fields will
be invalid.  You need to do a num,eric move to unpack it.
```

<a id="t-281cf4a322"></a>
## Thread: Follow up: Question for CICS gurus -- CICS CALL statements] — 4 msgs

_Topics: [`Mainframe, z/OS, JCL, CICS`](topics.md#mainframe)_

### Re: Follow up: Question for CICS gurus -- CICS CALL statements]

- **From:** colin <colin@light-ray.com>
- **Date:** 1998-07-04T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** alt.cobol,comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<359D82D7.3934951E@light-ray.com>`
- **References:** `<359BB445.FA569E2A@earthlink.com>`

```
SM wrote:

>  We have some success in running the program.  ( I have a feeling, its
> got something to do with compiler options....)
…[57 more quoted lines elided]…
>
 SM,
Ignore all prior posts except Mr Cleveland's; they're wrong. The
CICS reference manual clearly states that:
1. Calls made via a call (rather than an EXEC XCTL or LINK) <MUST> be
dynamic.
2. Where a working-storage area is used to contain the called program's
name it <MUST> have a length of PIC X(08).
3. If the CALLed routine is to access any CICS resources than the first
two parameters in the CALL list must be DFHEIB and DFHCOMMAREA.
I've mixed CALLs, XCTL and LINK since the v3.x days -- no problems.
Do, however, check the the Programmer's Guide for the compiler options
that CICS programs require.

regards,
```

#### ↳ Re: Follow up: Question for CICS gurus -- CICS CALL statements]

- **From:** Ken Foskey <waratah@zip.com.au>
- **Date:** 1998-07-06T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<35A0C3C5.ECA1F522@zip.com.au>`
- **References:** `<359BB445.FA569E2A@earthlink.com> <359D82D7.3934951E@light-ray.com>`

```

colin wrote:
> 
> SM wrote:
…[51 more quoted lines elided]…
> 

First I use static links exclusively in CICS.  This binds the object
into the load module and as far as CICS is concerned it is one program
not multiple routines.  It is funny that my DB2 get resolved to static
links.

Regarding dynamic links if you read the manually you will find that the
DYNAM option is how dynamic calls are made and not whether they are
made.  For example call 'fred' is static, call fred is a dynamic call.

To locate a dynamic call the steplib (do not know about CICS for this I
always use static) will be searched for a module.  If the module is
found then one of two things can happen 1:  it stores the name in a list
and calls it or 2:  it directly patches the called program with the
location of the routine.  If you are calling using constants defined in
working storage then you want 2 but if you are calling a routine name
that varies then you want 1.  Different modules can be called with
different optiuons and still work.

For example I worked on an personel admin package called CHRIS and it
made a called to a variable that was from XXXX01 through XXXX16 and
there were 16 identical modules with there own working storage keeping
the IO separate.  when compiled with the wrong option it called
exclusively XXXX01 (or whatever was called first).

Dynamic calls changed in MVS/390 (and YES 390 is a version of the OS as
well)  the compiler options are different especially with language
environment.  I just do not do this anymore.

I hope that this clears the dynamic calls up a bit more.  Over to the
CICS experts for a clearer picture of exactly what is needed.

Ken

PS:  there was another option that ties into the DYNAM option.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Follow up: Question for CICS gurus -- CICS CALL statements]

- **From:** "COBOLFrog (Huib Klink)" <H.Klink@IMN.nl>
- **Date:** 1998-07-06T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<35A0E562.346D2C7E@IMN.nl>`
- **References:** `<359BB445.FA569E2A@earthlink.com> <359D82D7.3934951E@light-ray.com> <35A0C3C5.ECA1F522@zip.com.au>`

```

Hmmm ..., Ken, I think you are a bit beside the truth.

Let's see, you wrote:
8< [snipped a lot]

> Regarding dynamic links if you read the manually you will find that the
> DYNAM option is how dynamic calls are made and not whether they are
> made.  For example call 'fred' is static, call fred is a dynamic call.

No. With NODYNAM, you are right: call 'fred' is static, call fred is a
dynamic call.
With DYNAM, you are wrong, then call 'fred' is dynamic and call fred is a
dynamic call.
8< [sniped another lot]

> PS:  there was another option that ties into the DYNAM option.

Wich one? Do you mean there is a tied option you can't remember, or are you
setting up a quiz?
:)

The COBOL Frog
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Follow up: Question for CICS gurus -- CICS CALL statements]

- **From:** mark0young@aol.com (Mark0Young)
- **Date:** 1998-07-07T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1998070705410500.BAA07869@ladder01.news.aol.com>`
- **References:** `<35A0C3C5.ECA1F522@zip.com.au>`

```

In article <35A0C3C5.ECA1F522@zip.com.au>, Ken Foskey <waratah@zip.com.au>
writes:

>Regarding dynamic links if you read the manually you will find that the
>DYNAM option is how dynamic calls are made and not whether they are
>made.  For example call 'fred' is static, call fred is a dynamic call.

No. DYNAM controls whether or not CALL 'FRED' is a static call or dynamic call.
If the compiler option is DYNAM, CALL 'FRED' will be a dynamic call and a load
module (VSE: FRED.PHASE) named FRED will be loaded at execution time. If the
compiler option is NODYNAM, CALL 'FRED' will be a static call and object module
FRED (VSE: FRED.OBJ) will be linked with the program at linkedit time.

CALL varname (where varname is a PIC X(8) field) will always be a dynamic call
and the load module / phase loaded at execute time will be the name contained
in that variable.

Mark A. Young
```

---

## Thread: How to get address of Cobol variable? — 1 msgs

_Topics: [`Help requests and how-to`](topics.md#help)_

### Re: How to get address of Cobol variable?

- **From:** Bill Lynch <wblynch@att.net>
- **Date:** 1998-07-04T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6nkbch$pd@bgtnsc01.worldnet.att.net>`
- **References:** `<01bda426$68ac5980$703d868b@nmc-pc> <1998070213375500.JAA18163@ladder01.news.aol.com> <359BACB4.6B6B@bt.com>`

```

Patrick Herring wrote:
> 
> S Comstock wrote:
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> 

The leftmost bit, when = 1, is a flag indicating a 31-bit address. If
you want to get rid of it, use "LA  Rx,0(,Rx)". We aren't limited to PIC
S9(8) on Big Blue, try "S9(9) BINARY SYNC" - no truncation there. I
haven't had a problem manipulating 31-bit addresses with this
specification.

Bill Lynch

> --
> ________________________________________________________
> Patrick Herring at work (clearly not - Ed.)
> Disclaimer: The appearance is BT but the essence is me.
> "Ockham's razor is so sharp, I bought the whole argument"

- **From:** dabevan@my-dejanews.com

01  ws-x.
      05  ws-y     pic 9(10) value 0.
      05  ws-z     pic 9(01) value 0.


move fielda to ws-x.

When I display the the 05 level fields, they are still packed. Could
this be a compiler problem?
```

#### ↳ Re: REPOST of signed field program

- **From:** "Carlos Garcia" <enigma@mail.telepac.pt>
- **Date:** 1998-07-06T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<01bda8be$ff8809c0$91b141c2@GASPE.DGASPE>`
- **References:** `<6nlfh2$e1t$1@ionews.ionet.net>`

```

Willie,

You can�t move a numeric packed field into a group level field. Cobol
doesn�t know the format of the destination field. You must first move the
packed field into a non packed field and then move the non packed field
into the group level field.
Following your example:

01 fieldb	pic S9(11) comp-3.


move fielda	to fieldb.
move fieldb	to ws-x.

That is a basic COBOL "thing". Any COBOL programmer knows that.
You must be a beginner. Sometimes people don�t answer to these kind of
questions because they feel, they are to "basic". But you got to start
somewhere! rigth ?
Hope to be helpfull.

Carlos Garcia.


Willie <wwalters@ionert.net> escreveu no artigo
<6nlfh2$e1t$1@ionews.ionet.net>...
>

>

Dave Johnson wrote:
> 
> ps - you must be working on kit older that yourself - thought they'd got rid
> of
> the CBLTDLI bit years ago (replaced it with EXEC DLI) - must be a pain
> being stuck with them linkage pointery things :-)

We have the "high level" (EXEC DLI) commands available, but our shop still
uses the old CALL CBLTDLI commands.  I experimented with using the EXEC DLI
commands about six months ago (as far as I could tell only one other person in
our shop even knew they existed, and even he never actually used them).  I
got both batch and CICS programs working using OS/VS COBOL, and CICS working
using COBOL II, but I never could get them working for batch programs using
COBOL II.

In any case, while it has some advantages, I found that I did not like it's
error handling.  The call stuff isn't all that bad, once you figure it out.

We are, BTW, a VSE shop...

>Fujitsu supports calling Entry Points in DLLs in two ways; static
assignment
>and dynamic assignment.  What Leif describes below is dynamic assignment.
>Resolution of the Entry Point is dynamically assigned at Runtime.  Static
>assignment is where you include the DLL's .LIB file at LINK time to
>statically assign the Entry Point.  There are benefits to both methods.
>Static assignment is the easiest method to use

I would LOVE to use the static method, but I find Chapter 10
incomprehensible.

I have a number of DLLs. They already exist, most written in a non-Cobol
language. How do I put them all in a LIB to use with the Fujitsu lib/linker
system to produce static linking? If I cannot do this, I cannot use static
assignment
as you call it.

Thanks

Fujitsu COBOL supports dynamic loading DLLs and also dynamic loading
multiple Entry Points in the DLL.  To dynamically call a DLL or Entry Point
in the DLL, use the DLOAD compiler directive.

Fujitsu COBOL V2 for Windows 3.1 (16-bit) includes Microsoft's 16-bit
IMPLIB.EXE utility.

For creating LIB files for Windows 95/NT (32-bit) see:
http://support.microsoft.com/support/kb/articles/q131/3/13.asp

Fujitsu Software Corporation
Developer Tools Group
Phone: (408) 428-0500
FAX:    (408) 428-0600
Email:  cobol@adtools.com
Web:    http://www.adtools.com

recommended.  My review of them show they will work.  Marshallsoft I

new state it, but you can't tell the INITIAL state.  

Anyway, I untimately make native windows API calls for the COM 
routines.

I like the Marshallsoft (sp) software better than what I did.  The 
price is not high, and there are no runtime royalties.  If it ever 
comes to the point that I need 32 bit com port routines, I'll get the 
Marshall soft routines.

(which is 16-bit) quite reliably under Win95.

I'm currently migrating the code to 32-bit but
using "C++ Builder" and using a comms-component
(in a "thread"). It appears to be working O.K.
(under Win95) in the limited testing I've yet been
able to give it. Using the A.P.I.'s seems straight
forward enough --- once I better get the "hang" of
C++.

This latter method (A.P.I.) would be the option
for the COBOL program the original poster enquired
about. A.P.I. use requires the provision of a
"call-back" routine as far as I'm aware but I
believe all that's necessary is the passing of the
address of the routine to the A.P.I. The routine
itself just accepts the data passed to it.

Cheers,

enabled the use of "callbacks". This way the
program does not have to POLL the serial port for
data but, instead, merely processes it in the
"callback" routine --- in the background as it
were. Windows calls your application when data is
available on the serial port.

32-bit.

(serial) "Comms" component which operates in its
own thread.

compiler arrived last week so it'll be interesting
doing serial comms under it --- when i get the
time to delve into it, that is.

<a id="t-90737e2d1e"></a>
## Thread: HELP! Can I Call the WINAPI to carry out native DOS commands??? — 8 msgs

_Topics: [`Help requests and how-to`](topics.md#help)_

### HELP! Can I Call the WINAPI to carry out native DOS commands???

- **From:** sglull@aol.com (Sglull)
- **Date:** 1998-07-06T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1998070614262600.KAA08195@ladder03.news.aol.com>`

```


I am running Microfocus Advanced Workbench under Windows NT 4.0

I am trying to find a way of  calling native DOS functions from within my COBOL
program.  I have been successful in using the Microfocus Library Call-Routines,
but some functions are not available.  For example, mapping a network drive,
doing a "dir" command and piping the results to a file, or changing the file
attributes(attrib).  

Does anyone have an example of  (or could point me in the direction to where I
may find information about) how to do this.  Can this be accomplished using the
WINAPI?  Could I create a "C" routine that I could call from my COBOL program
that would carry out these functions?  Can I write a COBOL routine that would
call a WINAPI (call-convention ??) to process the commands?  At the very least
I would like to suppress the DOS prompt from appearing.

Currently I am using the DISPLAY UPON COMMAND- LINE verbiage to accomplish the
task.  However, this works best if you are in a 16-bit environment; because if
you use this under NT 4.0, you get a DOS prompt box, or if you have multiple
DOS commands, you get a cascade of DOS prompt boxes. The prompts disappear
after they complete, but it's not esthetically pleasing.

Any comments or suggestions are welcome.

Thanks!
```

#### ↳ Re: HELP! Can I Call the WINAPI to carry out native DOS commands???

- **From:** Detlef Lexut <DLexut@gdtsoft.com>
- **Date:** 1998-07-07T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<35A22815.354A@gdtsoft.com>`
- **References:** `<1998070614262600.KAA08195@ladder03.news.aol.com>`

```

I have been successful in using the Microfocus Library 
Call-Routines, but some functions are not available.  For example, 
mapping a network drive, doing a "dir" command and piping the results to
a file, or changing the file attributes(attrib).

 Instead using the 'dir' command you can use the MF byte call x"91".
 DriveMapping and AttributeChange you can do with calling the WinApi's.
 Under Advanced WB call the 'COB32API', then you are able to call the 
 Windows API's. 

 good Luck
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: HELP! Can I Call the WINAPI to carry out native DOS commands???

- **From:** "Kevin J. Hansen" <screenio@pobox.alaska.net>
- **Date:** 1998-07-09T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<_v8p1.7$%02.235333@binary.alaska.net>`
- **References:** `<1998070614262600.KAA08195@ladder03.news.aol.com> <35A22815.354A@gdtsoft.com> <01bdaa48$caed2bb0$905481c1@gw-nt>`

```
You can also call WinExec, passing it the full path to COMMAND.COM, and
providing the /C command tail, which will fire up COMMAND.COM and tell it to
run the command appended to the /C.  One may venture that this is a more
"standard" method than using a Micro Focus call-by-number routine...

For example, to use a DOS command to format a floppy:

STRING
    'COMMAND.COM /CFORMAT A:'
    LOW-VALUE DELIMITED SIZE
        INTO WIN-COMMAND

CALL WINAPI 'WinExec' USING BY REFERENCE WIN-COMMAND
        BY CONTENT CMD-SHOW
        GIVING HINSTANCE.

CMD-SHOW specifies how the command window is to be run, e.g., minimized,
maximized, hidden, etc., and HINSTANCE is the instance handle of the spawned
process, which runs asynchronously.

FWIW, in our ScreenIO package, we're gradually bundling up and exporting
useful Windows APIs in a COBOL-friendly sort of way.  You just call our
entry point (in this example, it would be SCR_WINEXEC), passing the
arguments in the standard COBOL form, and we take care of rendering them to
the BY VALUE, BY REFERENCE form needed for the native C form call used by
Wndows.  This is much easier than wading through the C examples found in the
Windows SDK and trying to figure out how to translate them to COBOL

So, the call would be:

CALL 'SCR_WINEXEC' USING WIN-COMMAND
        CMD-SHOW
        HINSTANCE.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: HELP! Can I Call the WINAPI to carry out native DOS commands???

_(thread depth: 4)_

- **From:** "Donald Tees" <donald@willmack.com>
- **Date:** 1998-07-09T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6o3d53$6sm$1@news.igs.net>`
- **References:** `<1998070614262600.KAA08195@ladder03.news.aol.com> <35A22815.354A@gdtsoft.com> <01bdaa48$caed2bb0$905481c1@gw-nt> <_v8p1.7$%02.235333@binary.alaska.net>`

```
STRING
    'COMMAND.COM /CFORMAT A:'
    LOW-VALUE DELIMITED SIZE
        INTO WIN-COMMAND

Windows also has a "START.EXE" command that is a bit
more versatile than COMMAND.COM.  You can use it almost
the same, but it allows arguments that control parallelism a bit better.
The /? argument, under DOS, explains it all.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: HELP! Can I Call the WINAPI to carry out native DOS commands???

- **From:** gene.webb@mailexcite.com
- **Date:** 1998-07-13T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6oblco$cg9$1@nnrp1.dejanews.com>`
- **References:** `<1998070614262600.KAA08195@ladder03.news.aol.com> <35A22815.354A@gdtsoft.com>`

```
You can also make normal WIN32 API calls from COBOL.  It is just a matter of
using a calling-convention.  I believe the appropriate one is 66 like:

            call-convention 66 is WINAPI.

Then you can make most any WIN32 call that you want like:

            call WINAPI "SetForegroundWindow" using
                                              by value anId
                                    returning anOldId

The only trick is translating the 'C' data types to cobol.  Email me if you
decide to choose this route and I can send you a list of the translated data
types or you can use the MicroFocus H2CPY command on a 'C' .h file and it will
give you the most commonly used ones.




In article <35A22815.354A@gdtsoft.com>,
  Detlef Lexut <DLexut@gdtsoft.com> wrote:

…[10 more quoted lines elided]…

## Thread: Salary — 6 msgs

_Topics: [`Jobs, careers, recruiting, salary`](topics.md#jobs)_

### Salary

- **From:** Claus Heiko Niesen <cniesen@iastate.edu>
- **Date:** 1998-07-06T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<35A11500.487A@iastate.edu>`

```

I see that a few people seem to think that decent job offers post the
salary with them.

I am still a "baby-programmer" in the real world and don't have too much
experience with the job market.  All job offers that I have seen rarely
told the salary right away.  What quite often happened was that during
the interview the potential employer asked me what salary I wanted.  At
the time I was looking for my internship I didn't know and that's what I
told them. 

When a friend applied several years ago, another company asked him how
much he wanted during the interview.  I guess he asked for too much
since they weren't interested in him after that.


I would like to know how everybody on this news group feels about jobs
that are advertised without salaries.  And also if you think that quite
a few people aren't paid as much as they are worth because they didn't
ask for enough money.

Thanks
  Claus
```

Yes, I've seen this time and again.  This was especially true of really
good, sharp programmers back in the mid-eighties.  They just wouldn't
ask for enough money when they were providing astounding benefits
for management.  This is back when DP became MIS.

Management was making all manner of sound business decisions based
on the new stuff coming out of the computer room.  And in most cases
we weren't making much more than a journeyman TV tech.

I've since learned that you can pretty much get what you ask for.  I'm
very pleased with my compensation.  If you're a competent programmer,
there's no reason not to be pleased.

JC Jones.

## Thread: Q: building COBOL DLLs for use by C++ — 3 msgs

### Q: building COBOL DLLs for use by C++

- **From:** Andrew Caldwell <caldwell@panther.cs.ucla.edu>
- **Date:** 1998-07-06T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6nrkk2$463$1@news.cs.ucla.edu>`

```
Hi all-

I am trying to build a DLL in Cobol, and access it from C++.
It's not working very well. I'm usign Microfocus Object Cobol 4.0.
I can build DLLs with this, and have been able to access them 
in Cobol apps.  However, I can't even load the DLLs with my
C++ program (build w/ MSVC5.0).  I know it's not a path issue, 
as I can load other (C++ built) DLLs with the same (C++) code, from the
same directory.  I'm not even trying to call a function at this point..
just load the lib (one step at a time).  I expect issues such
as naming conventions are the problem, but haven't been able to find
much documentation on the issue.  Any suggestions would be greatly
appreciate.

thanks,
	-andy
	caldwell@cs.ucla.edu
```

#### ↳ Re: building COBOL DLLs for use by C++

- **From:** "NORCOM" <KHansen@norcomsoftware.com>
- **Date:** 1998-07-09T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<sQ8p1.8$%02.245235@binary.alaska.net>`
- **References:** `<6nrkk2$463$1@news.cs.ucla.edu>`

```
I suspect it is more likely a conflict with the Micro Focus runtime system
architecture.  Perhaps someone from Micro Focus will address this.

We have encountered a variety of problems with the MF runtime, depending
upon how you implemented your application.  We tested at least six different
combinations with our products, and found three that worked great and three
that failed in (usually subtle) ways.  Once you get it figured out, however,
it works fine.

But, again, I don't know about implementing a Windows DLL in Micro Focus
COBOL that could be called by someone else...

We do most of our development with CA-Realia.

We implemented our ScreenIO COBOL screen manager as a standalone Windows DLL
which is readily callable from any language, including at last count five
different COBOLs.  It would work just as well from C or VB, though I doubt
that anyone would want to do that.

We did it using CA-Realia II Workbench Version 3.0 (a 32-bit compiler).  It
was relatively easy, and it certainly made our life easier 'cause now we
only use one compiler for all development.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: building COBOL DLLs for use by C++

- **From:** "Gael Wilson" <gw@mfltd.co.uk>
- **Date:** 1998-07-10T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<01bdabd9$e1340e60$905481c1@gw-nt>`
- **References:** `<6nrkk2$463$1@news.cs.ucla.edu> <sQ8p1.8$%02.245235@binary.alaska.net>`

```
Andrew,

The same principle applies generally to COBOL DLLs as to the specific
example Norcom described below. A COBOL DLL is just an executable module so
it should be callable from any language that supports the same calling
mechanism. Micro Focus Object COBOL 4.0 actually uses Microsoft Visual C++
itself so there should certainly be no problems calling a COBOL DLL from
VC++ (in fact I know of many people who do exactly that). The default
calling convention for COBOL and the default for VC++ are in fact the same
so nothing special is needed in terms of entry points or function
declarations. That being the case you will probably need to provide a
little more information before I can help you get to the cause of the
problem.

1) Is the program created with VC++ actually a C++ program or a C program ?
If it is C++ you will need to define the COBOL as extern "C".

2) Do you reference the COBOL DLL by linking the C program with an import
library, or is it loaded via the LoadLibrary API ? If it is the latter,
what error is returned from GetLastError after the LoadLibrary fails ?

3) Have you checked that the COBOL run-time system (mfrts32.dll) can be
found ?

NORCOM <KHansen@norcomsoftware.com> wrote in article
<sQ8p1.8$%02.245235@binary.alaska.net>...
> I suspect it is more likely a conflict with the Micro Focus runtime
system
> architecture.  Perhaps someone from Micro Focus will address this.
> 
> We have encountered a variety of problems with the MF runtime, depending
> upon how you implemented your application.  We tested at least six
different

three
> that failed in (usually subtle) ways.  Once you get it figured out,
however,
> it works fine.
> 
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> We implemented our ScreenIO COBOL screen manager as a standalone Windows
DLL
> which is readily callable from any language, including at last count five
> different COBOLs.  It would work just as well from C or VB, though I
doubt
> that anyone would want to do that.
>

> was relatively easy, and it certainly made our life easier 'cause now we
> only use one compiler for all development.
…[27 more quoted lines elided]…

```

Dave Johnson wrote:

> COBOLFrog (Huib Klink) wrote in message <359C8385.C94EBBD1@IMN.nl>...

<a id="t-6a9ea82be8"></a>
## Thread: COBOL COPYBOOK — 1 msgs

### Re: COBOL COPYBOOK

- **From:** findit@end.of.message (Siegen)
- **Date:** 1998-07-06T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<359f7b83.19225649@news.comcen.com.au>`
- **References:** `<1998051502112800.WAA00804@ladder03.news.aol.com> <1998051503403000.XAA17845@ladder01.news.aol.com> <01bd8020$4adf5120$cef63bc0@mv-stevencc.mv.unisys.com> <6jr7gi$65v@sjx-ixn2.ix.netcom.com>`

```

"William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com> wrote:

:> Charles Stevens wrote in message
[snip]

:> >>   01  :PREFIX:-MASTER-REC.

:> >>  COPY bookname REPLACING ==:PREFIX:== BY ==M01==.
:> >> (The colons were used above to help set apart pieces 
:> >> that the REPLACING language could work on.)


:> >While some implementations would allow this, others would 
:> >not. Programs that make use of this feature may not be portable 
:> >to other systems.

:>   This is NOT true.  Using colons to delimit a prefix to 
:> be replaced will work with ANY compiler that conforms to 
:> the ANS'85 Standard.  It is true that older compilers 
:> (ANS'74 or earlier) will not accept this - but those
:> are pretty well disappearing as Y2K support becomes 
:> more important.

:> Bottom-line - the sample code will work and will be portable 
:> to any ANS'85 conforming compiler.
I still use the MicroSoft/MicroFocus Version 3.00
cobol compiler for D.O.S. 
It is set to use ANS85 features.

"COPY bookname REPLACING ==:PREFIX:== BY ==M01==."
This compiler rejects the colons and stops after
"swearing" at me in microsoftese.

If I omit the colons, it still won't do the
copy-replacing unless EACH LINE to be so modified
is expressly coded!! --- funnily enough though, it
compiles O.K. as far as when, in the "procedure
division", it encounters a use of the NEW field
name then stops after telling me that it has NOT
been declared.

e.g. of what works:

"COPY bookname REPLACING ==w100-field1== BY
==z999-field1=="

So, I have to do this for every field, therefore
it's easier just to create another library with
the fields already renamed to the new name.

Other ANS85 features work as they should.

(My "Fujitsu" compiler is much more civillised
though.)


-- Siegen 
---------------------- 
Please remove the letter "x" from my email address, should you wish
to email me.
(rodsp@comxcen.com.au)
---------------------
```

## Thread: Accessing A Modem From MF Cobol — 1 msgs

_Topics: [`Compilers and vendors`](topics.md#compilers)_

### Re: Accessing A Modem From MF Cobol

- **From:** "David Mowat" <dsmowat@clear.net.nz>
- **Date:** 1998-07-06T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6nq2r9$i16$1@fep5.clear.net.nz>`
- **References:** `<355ba18d.89040623@news.mci2000.com> <359dfeec.36304581@news.comcen.com.au>`

```

You can open a file whose file name is "Com2:"
Here is an example :-

SELECT COMM-STRING
   ORGANIZATION IS SEQUENTIAL
   ACCESS MODE IS SEQUENTIAL
   ASSIGN TO COMM-FILE-NAME
   FILE STATUS IS FILE-STATUS.

   FD COMM-STRING.
   01 C-STRING                   PIC X(80).

Regards ...
David Mowat
DSMowat Computer Consulting
D&M Computer & Technology Services Ltd
dsmowat@clear.cut.net.nz (remove cut)
If you want to start a Debt Collection Department or Agency, or go on the
Web, I have the Software.




>---- unless the
>COBOL open-file statement can be pointed at a
>COMMS port (I haven't tried that -- too obvious?)
>
```

## Thread: A Recruiter's View? — 6 this month / 7 total

_Topics: [`Jobs, careers, recruiting, salary`](topics.md#jobs)_

### A Recruiter's View?

- **From:** The Goobers <docdwarf@erols.com>
- **Date:** 1998-07-06T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.software.year-2000,comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<35A0A2E2.686A@erols.com>`

```

Well, gang... this *is* Bad Taste, I know... but here's an exchange I
had with a pimp regarding his refusal to post rates... it is a
cut-n-paste, his most recent comments are prefacte by the >:


--begin quote

Tom S Strutz wrote:
> 
> No, I will not post an amount.

Then by your actions you cut yourself off from the most desirable
applicants for your positions... such is your choice.

> Name one Fortune 1000 company that
> advertises openly the salaries for all full time postions in newspapers or
> the internet?

I did not know you were one of those... but this is what my grandmother
called the 'Brooklyn Bridge defense': 'So, everyone else is jumping off
the Brooklyn Bridge... you are, too?

> Yes, you may find high impact short supply positions or
> contract, but thats all.

Y2K is high impact.  Everyone's mentioned the Great Programmer Shortage.

> Furthermore the rates are going to drop.. Y2K work is drying up and rates
> will drop as H1B visas are expanded and more Indian and Asian programmers
> arrive.

If that happens, that happens... until then your actions are at best
premature, at worst inapropriate.

> Additionally,  a lot of 1999 budgets have been spent. IBM has
> signed little or no Y2K work and those thinking they are going to use tools

<a id="t-54e36f5a53"></a>
## Thread: BEA Tuxedo and NetExpress on Windows NT — 1 msgs

### BEA Tuxedo and NetExpress on Windows NT

- **From:** gene_webb@stercomm.com
- **Date:** 1998-07-06T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6nrmqs$dfl$1@nnrp1.dejanews.com>`

```

FYI,

For anyone that is using NetExpress on NT and is using or would like to use
MicroFocus COBOL.  They only support the Workbench product (which has really
been replaced by NetExpress) and I got an email from their technical support
saying they have no plans to change the COBOL support prior to the 2nd Half of
1999!

I was using Tuxedo and I am in the process of changing to Distributed CICS on
NT.  It is easier to administer than Tuxedo and it supports COBOL with
NetExpress including debugging of server and client applications.  Wow! What a
concept!

-----== Posted via Deja News, The Leader in Internet Discussion ==-----
http://www.dejanews.com/rg_mkgrp.xp   Create Your Own Free Member Forum

<a id="t-a2ce1ef3d8"></a>
## Thread: Euro Y2K COBOL Testing — 3 msgs

_Topics: [`Y2K and Year-2000 remediation`](topics.md#y2k)_

### Euro Y2K COBOL Testing

- **From:** Geoff Kennedy <geoffk@enterprise.net>
- **Date:** 1998-07-06T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<35A0E9AC.B9577ABD@enterprise.net>`

```

The year 2000 scenario raises issue that are unique and potentially will

trigger unqualifiable consequences.
Although the underlying problem is considered very basic, namely the
traditional method of entering and storing dates as 2 digits (98) rather

than 4 digits (1998) the issues are very profound:

   * The Problem is world-wide and universal across all computer
     manufacturers and system vendors.
   * The Impact will grow from now onwards reaching its peak on
     01.01.2000
   * The Deadline cannot be delayed
   * The Problem is compounded by affecting not only applications but
     the BIOS ( Basic Input/Output System ) Chips, operating systems as
     well as the application systems and there interfaces ( be it EDI as

     well as bespoke interfaces). This mixture generates almost an
     infinite number of scenarios, each with a potential unique
     consequence.

These consequences potentially may include

   * The Complete failure of systems
   * Inappropriate processing (i.e. reflecting all products as being
     "Out Of Date")
   * Rejection of valid information

Owing to the fact that system consequences are not easily predictable,
the business consequences are also unpredictable, (i.e. inability for
customers to order or pay; suppliers to deliver) therefor business
contingency also needs to be considered.

If you have not created a year 2000 task force, do so today with
properly qualified personnel. If you do have a year 2000 task force,
ensue an immediate audit of its procedures, activities and plans

If you want any help or assistance please contact Euro-Y2K, we will
respond immediately.

Euro Y2K can offer a In-house training, a full assessment of your
Business or task force, we can set up a test facility and potentially
save you ï¿½ï¿½ï¿½ï¿½'s with features such as our ability to test and correct an

infinite number of COBOL lines, very quickley

for more details please contact geoffk@enterprise.net
```

## Thread: Question for CICS gurus -- CICS CALL statements — 2 msgs

_Topics: [`Mainframe, z/OS, JCL, CICS`](topics.md#mainframe)_

### Re: Question for CICS gurus -- CICS CALL statements

- **From:** "Your Name" <PatronID@glaxowellcome.com>
- **Date:** 1998-07-06T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol,alt.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<01bda8fd$e6656980$c58c3398@us0071826>`
- **References:** `<359AC0B4.F0A9B825@earthlink.com>`

```

I have a similar problem once. It has something to do with how CICS/COBOL
II pass
its working storage address to the called program. COBOL II/CICS programs
cannot call
on OS/VS Cobol programs. Cobol II being "above the 16MB line". Check if
your batch 
COBOL program if it is COBOL II or not.

Aedehl

SM <manjigani@earthlink.com> wrote in article
<359AC0B4.F0A9B825@earthlink.com>...
> Hi,
> 
…[19 more quoted lines elided]…
>
```

#### ↳ Re: Question for CICS gurus -- CICS CALL statements

- **From:** "AM" <AM69107@glaxowellcome.com>
- **Date:** 1998-07-06T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol,alt.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<01bda90c$4fbcb1f0$c58c3398@us0071826>`
- **References:** `<359AC0B4.F0A9B825@earthlink.com> <01bda8fd$e6656980$c58c3398@us0071826>`

```

hope these pages from the IBM manuals help you find your answer. That is if
your called batch program is OS/VS Cobol.... being called from COBOL
II/CICS.
that was the situation I was in on a previous project, and what we did is
compiled
the program under COBOL II and use that object on our CICS/COBOL II
programs.

from the IBM Manuals: Programming Guide

In addition to making calls between COBOL for MVS & VM programs, you can
also
make static and dynamic calls between COBOL for MVS & VM and VS COBOL II
programs and, in a non-CICS environment, between COBOL for MVS & VM and
OS/VS COBOL programs. In a CICS environment, you must use EXEC CICS LINK
to transfer control between COBOL for MVS & VM and OS/VS COBOL programs.

When to Use a Dynamic Call
Use a dynamic call statement when any of the following are true:
o  You are concerned about ease of maintenance. Applications do not have to
be
    link-edited again when dynamically called subprograms are changed.

o  The subprograms called are used infrequently or are very large.
    If the subprograms are called on only a few conditions, dynamic calls
can bring
    in the subprogram only when needed.
    If the subprograms are very large or there are many of them, using
static calls 
    might require too much main storage. Less total storage might be
required to
    call and cancel one, then call and cancel another, than to statically
call both.

o  You want to call subprograms in their unused state, and you cannot use
the
    INITIAL attribute.

    When you cannot use the INITIAL attribute to ensure that a subprogram
is
    placed in its unused state each time it is called, you can set the
unused state
    by using the CALL and CANCEL procedure that is described next. (This is
a
    more cumbersome and inefficient procedure, but does provide control of
the
    state, if that is essential).

    For this procedure, use a combination of dynamic CALL and CANCEL
state-ments.
    When you CANCEL the subprogram that was first called by an OS/VS
    COBOL, VS COBOL II or COBOL for MVS & VM program, the next CALL will
    cause the subprogram to be reinitialized to its unused state.

    Using the CANCEL statement to explicitly cancel a subprogram that was
    dynamically loaded and branched to by a non-COBOL program does not
result
    in any action being taken to release the subprogram's storage or to
delete the
    subprogram.

o  You have an OS/VS COBOL or other AMODE(24) program in the same run
    unit with COBOL for MVS & VM programs that you want to run in 31-bit
    addressing mode. OS/VS COBOL, VS COBOL II, and COBOL for MVS & VM
    dynamic CALL processing include AMODE switching for AMODE(24) programs
    calling AMODE(31) programs, and vice versa. To have this implicit AMODE
    switching done, you must use the Language Environment run-time option,
    ALL31(OFF). AMODE switching is not performed when ALL31(ON) is set. For
    details on the ALL31 run-time option, see  Language Environment
Programming
    Reference.

    When AMODE switching is performed, control is passed from the caller to
a
    Language Environment library routine. After the switching is performed,
control
    is passed to the called program, and the library routine's save area
will be posi-tioned 
    between the caller program's save area and called program's save area.

o  The program name to be called is not known until run time.
         � Here, use the format CALL  identifier, where the  identifier is
a data item that
            will contain the name of the called program at run time. In
terms of prac-tical 
           application, you might use CALL  identifier when the program to
be
           called is variable, depending on conditional processing in your
program.
         � CALL  identifier is always dynamic, even if you use the NODYNAM
compiler
            option.

Calls under CICS
If your COBOL program runs under CICS, observe these CALL restrictions and
requirements:
o   You can make calls to and from VS COBOL II and/or COBOL for MVS & VM
     programs.
o   Assembler-language programs that conform to the interface described in
the
     Language Environment Programming Guide are called LE-conforming
assem-bler 
     programs. Ones that do not conform to the interface can be called
     non-LE-conforming assembler programs.

    A COBOL for MVS & VM program can call a non-LE-conforming
assembler-language 
    program. However, a non-LE-conforming assembler program cannot
    call a COBOL for MVS & VM or a VS COBOL II program.

o  Calls to LE-conforming assembler programs are supported. Calls from
    LE-conforming assembler programs are supported with the restriction
that the
    assembler-language program is not a main program.

o  The NODYNAM compiler option must be used if the COBOL program has been
    translated by the CICS translator.

o  CALL  identifier can be used with the NODYNAM compiler option to
dynamically
    call a program. Called programs can contain any function supported by
CICS
    for the language. Dynamically called programs have to be defined in the
CICS
    program processing table (PPT).

o  If you are calling a COBOL program that has been translated, you must
pass
    DFHEIBLK and DFHCOMMAREA as the first two parameters in the CALL
    statement.

****************************************************************************
***********
o  COBOL for MVS & VM and VS COBOL II programs cannot call or be called by
    OS/VS COBOL programs. EXEC CICS LINK must be used instead.
****************************************************************************
***********

o  Support for interlanguage communication (ILC) with other HLL languages
is
    available. For more detailed information on ILC, see  Language
Environment
    Programming Guide. Where ILC is not supported, you can use CICS LINK,
    XCTL, and RETURN instead.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: CICS 4.1 HELP PLEASE

- **From:** "AM" <AM69107@glaxowellcome.com>
- **Date:** 1998-07-07T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<01bda9b3$c1bfe220$c58c3398@us0071826>`
- **References:** `<3587C98D.E666AAC7@cslp.org> <01bda916$fb6fe170$c58c3398@us0071826> <6ns5l5$nsh@bgtnsc03.worldnet.att.net>`

```
Yes OS/VS COBOL is below the line. and passing the working storages to an
above the line
program such as COBOL II was (one of) the cause(s) of the problem we had.
You cannot use a CALL
statement between OS/VS COBOL and higher COBOL versions (COBOL II / MVS
COBOL) in
a CICS environment.

## Thread: Known problems with Y2K SEEC product — 1 msgs

_Topics: [`Y2K and Year-2000 remediation`](topics.md#y2k)_

### Re: Known problems with Y2K SEEC product

- **From:** SM <manjigani@earthlink.com>
- **Date:** 1998-07-06T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** alt.cobol,comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<35A15A13.41ACF9F@earthlink.com>`
- **References:** `<359E710E.60902490@with.spammers.com> <6nlsiq$218@bgtnsc01.worldnet.att.net>`

```
What version of SEEC are you using????

I have been using SEEC (till ACCUFIND part), and fixing with a different
tool.  It usually gives a lot of false positives, particularly after ADD
Indirects.... (This is really pain in the ****).

But regarding your problem, I had many instances of hardcoded 19s and 20s,
and never it failed to capture them.  Also, it flags all the move statements
with years in leading position (A century neighbour concept, as SEEC calls).

I think you need to do more work on rules file and cleaning up the initial
seeds / dsv file(like any of these new tools).

Bill Lynch wrote:

> No Spam wrote:
> >
…[23 more quoted lines elided]…
> > address, so I've had to resort to doing this.
```

Back in 1964 when I was with CDC and assigned to NV Phillips in 
Eindhoven Nederland to help them with COBOL, I was asked to change all 
the reserved words in the COBOL compiler to Dutch words.  I explained 
that this makes no sense because the programs would never be portable 
and so on.  Also, the languages are constructed differently with verbs 
in different places.  Just changing the words wouldn't do it.  They 
gave in.
```

### FD vs W/S

them in the FD section.  I vaguely remember some problem with loosing the
data in the FD section, while data in working-storage stays put.  Does
anyone know what I'm talking about?  (I'm working with COBOL for MVS a.k.a.
COBOL III).

#### ↳ Re: FD vs W/S

- **From:** mark0young@aol.com (Mark0Young)
- **Date:** 1998-07-08T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1998070804295400.AAA29756@ladder01.news.aol.com>`
- **References:** `<6nuibt$cs6$1@birch.prod.itd.earthlink.net>`

```
In article <6nuibt$cs6$1@birch.prod.itd.earthlink.net>, "Mike Revels"
<revelsm@juno.com> writes:

>
>I've been wracking my brain recently, trying to remember why I got into the
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>COBOL III).

This discussion is from an IBM mainframe perspective, i.e, MVS & VSE
implementations.

In case I mix "block" and "buffer", a "buffer" is an area of memory that (in
this context) will hold a record (unblocked files and unit-record files such as
card readers, card punches, and line printers) or a "buffer" will hold the
equivalent of a disk block of data (for blocked files, typical of disk and
tape). For example, if a file is defined with a record size of 80 and a block
size of 23440 (MVS terminology: DCB=(LRECL=80,BLKSIZE=23440,RECFM=FB)), a
buffer of 23440 bytes will be used (double-buffering: two buffers of 23440
bytes each), and the data in the buffer, when written, will write a disk block
of 23440 bytes, or the equivalent of slighly half of half of a 3380 track. So
one could say that the buffer "holds a block of data".

Typically, records defined in the FD are actually pointers into a buffer, and
the buffer is accessed in locate mode, meaning that the 01 in the FD is set to
the address of the logical record being processed.

For unit record devices (card reader, line printer), if double buffering is
used, the FD's 01 alternates between the two buffers, e.g., when the file is
opened, two buffers are allocated (if not already reserved) and the 01 is set
to point to the first buffer. The values are moved to it, a WRITE causes a
Start I/O operation (or EXCP) and the 01 is moved to the other buffer. (In
COBOL terms, a WRITE FD_rec_name FROM ws_rec_name is equivalent to a MOVE
ws_rec_name to FD_rec_name followed by a WRITE FD_rec_name.) Data is moved to
the buffer that the 01 now points to and when the second WRITE is issued, the
job waits until the first SIO completes, then the 01 is moved to the first
buffer and a SIO is issued against the second buffer. In this way (using
double-buffering), one of the buffers can be in the process of being filled
while the other buffer is tied up with I/O, and the two buffers alternate
roles. The theory here is that the program can be doing serious work and an I/O
can be taking place at the same time, producing a net throughput improvement
over a single-buffering situation.

The practical side is that after moving values into an 01, after a WRITE is
issued, the 01 no longer points to the same buffer, so one cannot look at the
01 to see what the value was that was just written.

CLOSE guarantees that the SIO operation will be allowed to complete before the
program is deallocated and memory (including the buffers) are deallocated.

Single buffering seems more reasonable because the 01 for a unit-record device
would point to just one buffer. But in this case, once values are moved to the
01 and a WRITE is issued, the program is suspended while the SIO is taking
place and the program is not allowed to continue until the I/O operation is
posted as completed. If dealing with real devices (instead of a spooler
system), there would be no overlap between processing and I/O (the processing
would be suspended until I/O completes), so the overall runtime of the job
would be increased.

SAM files on disk or tape, unblocked, single or double buffered, would run
typically as described above.

For SAM files (disk or tape), it is common for the file to be blocked. At OPEN
time, one, two, or (MVS) more buffers are set aside for I/O, and the FD's 01 is
set to point to the start of the first block. Let's consider two buffers. When
data is moved into the 01 it is actually being moved into the first area of the
buffer. (If the 01 record is 80 bytes long, data would be moved into the first
80 bytes of the buffer.) When a logical WRITE is issued, the 01 is moved to the
next area in the buffer. (E.g., the 01 is moved 80 bytes into thebuffer.) When
there have been enough logical WRITEs that the entire buffer has been filled,
the 01 is moved to the start of the second buffer of memory that was set aside
for I/O, and a Start I/O operation (SIO or EXCP) is started to physically write
the first buffer to disk or tape. As additional logical writes take place, the
second buffer is finally filled, at which time (for double buffering) the
program is suspended until the first buffer had finished being written to disk
or tape. Once the SIO for the first block has been posted as completed, an SIO
is started to write the second buffer of data, the 01 is updated to point to
the start of the first buffer of data, and the program is allowed to resume.

By blocking data (having multiple records saved into a buffer so that when an
SIO starts, the block of data it writes contains multiple logical records),
fewer SIO operations (VSE terminology: EXCPs) are required for writing the same
amount of data, so less operating system overhead is required, fewer tape
start-stop operations are required, fewer inter-block gaps are used (both disk
and tape), with the result that data density (disk and tape) is generally
significantly better and the amount of time spent doing physical I/O and
supervisor overhead for I/O is significantly decreased.

However, that means when a COBOL program does a WRITE, the value just written
will not be available in the 01 again until several or even hundreds of WRITE
operations later.

A CLOSE operation will check to see if the buffer the 01 currently points to
has had one or more logical WRITEs issued to it since the 01 was readdressed to
it and, if so, will write the buffer out to disk (typically a short block,
i.e., the block written will be the size of the buffer that the I/O routines
think were actually used), as well as guarantee that the most recent SIO
completes before control goes back to the program.

I haven't covered more-than-2 buffers (buffers can be filled further in advance
of the SIOs but still an SIO will have to be completed before a buffer can be
reused), nor have I covered VSAM (read-only non-spanned: 01 points to the
buffer holding the data CI and the 01's pointer is updated for each sequential
READ; for spanned records or output: a secondary buffer the size of the largest
possible record as defined in the VSAM catalog is allocated and the 01 points
to that buffer, but it is not recommended to rely on this particular behavior).

On input files, the 01 may point to an "indeterminate location" once an
end-of-file is reached, so it is a bad practice to move anything to the FD/01
of an input file, especially once EOF (sequential) or INVALID KEY (random read)
has been reached.

The story is completely different for WORKING-STORAGE. The behavior of
WORKING-STORAGE is very deterministic: the location does not change for the
duration of the program. If one does a WRITE ... FROM ws_rec_name, the value of
ws_rec_name will not be changed by the WRITE (unlike what the value of the 01
because the address of what the 01 points to can be modified on every WRITE).
That means that after the WRITE ... FROM ws_rec_name, you can still access the
values just written by examining ws_rec_name. Likewise, when doing a READ ...
INTO ws_rec_name, you are allowed to make subsequent changes to values in
ws_rec_name, even when AT END (i.e., end-of-file) has been encountered. (A
common trick for merges is to move high values into the key of the
WORKING-STORAGE layout for the file whose eof one just encountered--you cannot
do that to the FD/01, but you can to the WS/01.)

Another benefit of having output record layouts be in working storage is that
one can use VALUE clauses or do once-only MOVEs of initial values to that
record layout, and it will stay there. (FD/01 VALUE clauses, except for 88s,
are treated as comments or warnings.)

My preference is to have record descriptions in working storage. In olden days
when one got hex dumps, it was a lot easier to see what record one was
currently working on when it was in WORKING-STORAGE because it would be in a
specific location, but one would have to decode control blocks if one had to
look at the file buffers to see what record the program was working on at abend
time. This probably isn't so important today when using COBOL/VSE or COBOL/MVS
and the Language Environment, and the code had been compiled with CBL
TEST(,SYM) because then the Language Environment dump routines would (should?)
figure out which record in the file buffers was the currently active record
(i.e., which record the 01 was really pointing at). Oh yes, I remember coding
all kinds of eye catchers, e.g.,

  01 FILLER PIC X(32) VALUE 'WORKING STORAGE STARTS HERE:'.
  ...
  01 FILLER PIC X(16) VALUE 'INPUT RECORD:'.
  01 WS-IN-REC.
       ....
but those eye-catchers aren't needed with TEST(NONE,SYM).

Even so, for debugging purpose, it is critical that one is able to look at the
raw, unadulterated input record currently being processed, so if _any_
modification of the input record takes place, it is better (for debugging) if
the record is moved into WORKING-STORAGE (e.g., READ ... INTO) and the
WORKING-STORAGE version be modified, while letting the FD/01 remain as the
virgin record image.

Mark A. Young
```

#### ↳ Re: FD vs W/S

- **From:** "Dennis J. Minette" <d.minette@computer.org>
- **Date:** 1998-07-09T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<#I0ocW7q9GA.220@upnetnews03>`
- **References:** `<6nuibt$cs6$1@birch.prod.itd.earthlink.net>`

```
You probably got into the habit of working with data in working storage
because some wise, experienced programmer most likely told you, way back
when you were starting out as a rookie programmer, that you should "NEVER
work in the buffer". This was (is!) good advice because data that was in a
record buffer before a WRITE was not necessarily there after a WRITE.  Many
COBOL compiler manuals have stated that the contents of the FD 01 were to be
considered "undefined" after a WRITE operation.

>data in the FD section, while data in working-storage stays put.  Does
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>
```

Another problem with using an FD area after a WRITE is that
the undefined nature means that the fields will be intact for 99%
of the time and the inexperienced programmer will begin to
ASS u ME that in fact that was an old fogy's notion and it's ok to
add, subtract, compare etc on fields after a WRITE op.

Then the overlay gets changed, or some memory is added to
the machine, or the moon is full, and the inexperienced programmer
becomes an older and wiser programmer, ususally around 2:00am.

Gotcha.

James Jones.

I know this is off the subject of COBOL (sort of).

I have been considering "Moonlighting" by working on COBOL programs during
my "OFF" hours.   Are there any of you who do this kind of thing?

During the day, I am a web application programmer (no COBOL there) and would
like to keep my skills up in COBOL.  Primarily because I teach COBOL at the
Community College level and do not want to get too rusty.

Did you need the assistance of a lawyer?

What TAX considerations did you need to make?

How do you "collect" on finished projects?

Do you find it easy to get in OVER your head?
